# Decision-Making Discipline

Good engineering decisions are rarely about finding a perfect option. They are about choosing a direction with clear evidence, known tradeoffs, and a reversal story.

## When to Decide

Decide only after the choice boundary is clear:

- What changes depending on the decision?
- What evidence would make one option clearly better?
- What is the cost of delaying the decision?
- What is the cost of being wrong?

If the cost of being wrong is low and the cost of analysis is high, choose the simplest reversible option. If the decision changes contracts, data, security posture, or user-visible behavior, slow down and collect stronger evidence.

## Compare Options

For each real option, state:

- The problem it solves.
- The complexity it adds.
- The behavior it preserves.
- The behavior it changes.
- How it fails.
- How it can be backed out.

Do not compare an implemented option against a vague ideal. Compare concrete options that could actually be built now.

## Reversibility

Prefer decisions that can be reversed locally:

- A helper function is easier to reverse than a public API change.
- A feature flag is easier to reverse than a data migration.
- A narrow adapter is easier to reverse than touching every caller.

When a decision is hard to reverse, write down the reason it is still worth making.

## Reporting Decisions

When explaining a decision, lead with the chosen option and the evidence that made it preferable. Mention rejected options only if the reader would otherwise ask why they were not chosen.

The strongest decision record is short: "Chose X because Y was observed; rejected Z because it would change A and B; reversal is C."
